This transformation is underpinned by the radical disruption of neural voice synthesis and the emergence of "Agentic Commerce," where AI-driven discoverability dictates consumer behavior. For an enterprise aiming to deploy a catalog of 2,160 titles—structured as twenty distinct niche silos each containing 108 books—the intersection of administrative precision and persona-driven content strategy is paramount. The following analysis provides an exhaustive roadmap for executing this high-volume deployment, leveraging advanced AI narration, strategic duration optimization, and multi-platform distribution protocols.Market Dynamics and the Scaling ImperativeThe 2026 market is defined by a shift toward digital-centric workflows and the democratization of production. The historical barriers to entry—primarily the high cost and long turnaround times of human narration—have been neutralized. Whereas traditional professional narration could cost up to $4,000 for a ten-hour project, modern AI synthesis allows for equivalent production at a cost reduction of over 90%, often totaling less than $200 per title. This economic shift enables the "niche hunger" strategy, where profitability is achieved not through singular bestsellers but through the aggregation of micro-niche sales across a massive catalog.A critical component of this strategy is the understanding of consumer psychology. Listeners in 2026 are predominantly multitaskers who consume content while commuting, exercising, or performing household tasks. This demographic favors "practical and productive" content, specifically in the non-fiction and self-improvement sectors, which age more slowly than fiction and allow backlist titles to generate recurring revenue over years. For the proposed 2,160-book deployment, this longevity is essential for recouping administrative and production costs.Strategic Persona Alignment and Niche MappingThe foundation of the deployment rests on the "Unified Persona System," which consolidates legacy professional and lifestyle profiles into targeted actionable segments. With this model, authors release shorter installments that can be monetized as ad-supported audio, through membership communities like Patreon, or as individual retail titles. For a 108-book silo, serialization allows for a "compound interest" effect in marketing; as listeners finish Book 1 of a series, the platform's metadata automatically surfaces Book 2, increasing the Lifetime Value (LTV) of a single customer acquisition.The mathematics of production for these series chunks relies on a standardized "Word-to-Hour" ratio. The industry standard remains approximately 9,300 words per finished hour. 2) Automatic Title Generator (Rules-based) Spec
Goal
Generate 9 titles + subtitles per series that:
match persona tone
follow a consistent series intent arc
avoid platform rejection language
avoid duplication across catalog
Inputs
admin_id, persona_id, series_id
series_goal
book_count (always 9)
series_intent_tags (e.g. burnout, boundaries, recovery)

global forbidden words list
persona forbidden words list
existing titles index (for dedupe)
Outputs
For each book 1–9:
title.main
title.subtitle
title.seo_keywords (optional internal)
title.fingerprint (for collision checks)
Title “arc” template (9-book progression)
Book numbers map to intent:
Naming the problem (clarity)
Understanding mechanisms (how it works)
Common traps (what keeps it going)
First shift (reframe)
Practice (skill)
Boundaries (application)
Resilience (stability)
Integration (identity)
Long-view mastery (leadership / stewardship)
Persona style profiles (examples)
Healthcare RN: soothing, steady, care-forward
NYC Exec: systems, consequence, precision
SF Founder/Dev: process, reality, technical clarity
Parents: calm authority, safety, guidance
Forbidden language enforcement
Hard fail if title/subtitle contains:
guarantees, cure, instant, secret, act now, “in X days”, “without effort”
medical claims (cure depression, treat PTSD)
financial promises (get rich, guaranteed returns)
Collision avoidance
normalize(title+subtitle) → 6-gram hash index
reject if collision > threshold
reject if same leading bigram appears too often within a series
Pseudocode (rules-based, deterministic)


def generate_series_titles(series_blueprint, existing_index, seed):
    rng = DeterministicRNG(seed)  # stable across reruns
    arc = ARC_INTENTS_9
    titles = []
    used_roots = set()

    for i, intent in enumerate(arc, start=1):
        candidates = expand_patterns(
            persona=series_blueprint.persona,
            series_id=series_blueprint.series_id,
            intent=intent,
            metaphor_bank=series_blueprint.metaphors,
            verb_bank=series_blueprint.verbs,
            noun_bank=series_blueprint.nouns,
        )

        # enforce constraints + dedupe
        candidates = [
            c for c in candidates
            if passes_forbidden_words(c)
            and passes_length(c)
            and not collides(existing_index, c)
            and root_not_overused(used_roots, c)
        ]

        chosen = deterministic_pick(rng, candidates)
        titles.append(chosen)
        used_roots.add(extract_root(chosen))

    return titles

Storage
Generated titles are written into BookPlans before freeze
After freeze, titles are immutable.

what is the overall strategy wth authors in the systerm

Here’s the overall author strategy in the system:
1. Persona → author pool (content/UI)
Selection order: Platform → Persona → Author → Topic → Source → Assembly style → Duration.
Author list comes from persona:
unified_personas.yaml defines each persona’s author_pool_file (and sometimes author_pool_files_by_topic), e.g.:
healthcare_rns → authors/generated/healthcare_rns_authors.yaml
gen_z_professionals → authors/generated/gen_z_professionals_authors.yaml
gen_alpha_students → authors/generated/gen_alpha_students_authors.yaml
Plus region-specific pools (e.g. atlanta, chicago, cincinnati).
Fallback: If a persona has no pool (or it fails to load), the UI falls back to opinion_gen_z_authors.yaml or opinion_gen_alpha_authors.yaml and can show a warning.
So authors are persona-scoped: you don’t pick “any author,” you pick an author from the pool of the chosen persona.
2. Author = content identity + voice + cover identity
Each author is one consistent “voice” used across:
Content: Name, bio, domains, tones, POV (e.g. first-person for “opinion” authors).
Narration: voice_profile_id (and narrator name) for TTS.
Covers: Same author_id is used by the cover engine (see below).
When a book/manifest is created, a minimal author snapshot is stored: author_id, voice_profile_id, narrator, pov, author_pool_file. That snapshot is what flows into assembly and export.
3. Cover engine: one strategy per author (ACGS)
Idea: Each author has a single, locked “Cover Growth Strategy” so their covers evolve in a consistent, deterministic way and different authors look different.
Three strategies (V1 locked):
Single overlay accumulation – calm, minimal (e.g. Gen Z mental health).
Multi-overlay rhythmic – layered, dynamic (e.g. Gen Alpha learning/focus).
Panel + mask composer – structured, graphic (e.g. career/authority).
Assignment: Inferred from persona + topics when the author is onboarded (e.g. Gen Z + mental health → single overlay; Gen Alpha + learning → multi-overlay; career/authority → panel/mask).
Onboarding: When authors are created (e.g. by generate_persona_authors.py or add_expert_authors.py), they are auto-onboarded to the cover engine: persona is inferred, a strategy is assigned, and covers/authors/{author_id}/ is populated (profile, strategy lock, background prefs, entropy history). Already-onboarded authors are skipped.
Backgrounds: Tier 2 topic backgrounds are per author: covers/backgrounds/tier_2_topic/{author_id}/. The manifest’s author_id is what the cover compiler uses to pick that author’s pool.
So the “strategy with authors” for covers is: one author_id → one locked cover strategy and one background pool, chosen by persona/topic at onboarding and then fixed.
4. Where authors are created and stored
Defined in: authors/generated/*.yaml (and similar), keyed by author_id.
Registry: authors/registry.yaml points some persona/topic combos to specific generated files (e.g. healthcare_rns anxiety/grief, gen_alpha/gen_z anxiety/grief).
Cover side: covers/authors/{author_id}/ holds the cover profile and strategy for that author. The same author_id is used in manifests and in paths like tier_2_topic/{author_id}/.
5. End-to-end flow (summary)
Personas in unified_personas.yaml point to author pool YAMLs.
UI shows authors from the selected persona’s pool; user picks one → author_id (+ voice, POV, etc.) is stored in the manifest.
New authors (from generation scripts) are auto-onboarded to the cover engine and get a strategy and covers/authors/{author_id}/.
Assembly/export (e.g. Phoenix V4) use manifest author_id for voice, intro/outro, and cover (tier_2_topic and strategy).
Covers evolve deterministically per author (book position + strategy), with no extra per-book strategy choice.
So the overall strategy with authors is: authors are the main identity handle — they’re scoped by persona, carry content + voice + POV, and each has a single, locked cover strategy and background set so the system can scale to many authors while keeping each one consistent and distinct.
in v4 are we addressing good: [When it comes to **self-help audiobooks**, *who the author appears to be* matters a **lot** — because readers and listeners don’t just want ideas and advice, they want to *trust* the person delivering them. That trust influences whether they *believe the content*, *feel connected*, and *recommend it to others*. ([The Self-Publishing Advice Center][1])



Here’s what really matters about the author in self-help and how you can shape that perception — even in an AI-assisted system:



---



## 🧠 **1. Credibility and Expertise**



People choose self-help authors because they seem like someone who *knows what they’re talking about*. That can come from:



* **Real credentials** (education, certifications, experience in the topic)

* **Relevant life experience** (stories of personal growth or overcoming challenges)

* **Clear, thoughtful insights** that feel grounded and practical. ([Gotham Ghostwriters][2])



**Why this matters:** If the author *feels real*, listeners are more willing to engage with the ideas and take them seriously.



---



## 🤝 **2. Authentic Personal Voice**



In self-help, listeners want to *feel heard*. That requires a voice that is:



* **Human and personal**

* **Emotionally honest and present**

* **Consistent in tone and values**



Even if an author is fictional or algorithmically generated, the writing needs to sound like a real *person with depth*, not a generic AI voice. ([The Self-Publishing Advice Center][1])



👉 A strong author voice feels like someone the listener would *want to meet in real life*.



---



## 📖 **3. Experience and Stories**



Good self-help books are full of **stories and examples** — not just abstract advice. These can include:



* Personal anecdotes

* Case studies

* Illustrative scenarios



These aren’t just filler — they make abstract concepts *feel real* and *relatable*. ([Gotham Ghostwriters][2])



---



## 📣 **4. Transparency and Trust**



There’s a growing concern in the publishing world about *AI content and authenticity*. Some publishers and author communities encourage:



* **Transparency about AI usage**

* Human editing and curation

* Genuine author involvement in the writing process



Because if readers suspect something is *inauthentic or deceptive*, it can *hurt credibility*. ([The Self-Publishing Advice Center][1])



---



## 📌 **So what should you do to “look good” with made-up authors in your AI book system?**



### ✅ **A. Build believable, consistent author backstories**



Create short biographies that include:



* Relevant *theoretical* credentials (e.g., “10 years coaching mindfulness”)

* Realistic *experience journeys*

* Specific *specialties and perspectives*



Make them feel like *real professionals*.



### ✅ **B. Use clear, human-sounding voice and personality**



Avoid overly generic phrasing. Add:



* First-person reflections (“When I worked with X, I noticed…”)

* Thoughtful, specific insights

* Natural emotional nuance



These make the content feel authored by a *thoughtful expert*, not a robot.



### ✅ **C. Include real-world examples + stories**



Even fictional authors benefit from:



* Well-crafted case examples

* Narrative scenarios

* Analogies that resonate emotionally



This adds depth and trust.



### ✅ **D. Be thoughtful about AI transparency**



Even if the author is fictional:



* Consider disclaimers or a note about AI assistance

* Make it clear the ideas are *curated for the listener’s benefit*



This preserves ethics and trust. ([The Self-Publishing Advice Center][1])



---



## 🧩 Summary — What Really Matters



| What Matters       | Why It Matters                       |

| ------------------ | ------------------------------------ |

| Credibility        | Makes listeners trust the guidance   |

| Authentic voice    | Helps connection and engagement      |

| Stories & examples | Makes concepts real and memorable    |

| Transparency       | Protects trust and ethical integrity |



---



If your system uses *made-up* authors, focus on making their **voice, backstory, and advice feel genuinely human and responsibly grounded** — that’s what makes listeners *buy in* and *stay engaged*. ([The Self-Publishing Advice Center][1])

Checking how V4 models authors and where it aligns with or falls short of those principles.
Here’s how V4 (and the wider system) line up with those self-help author-trust ideas, and what would make it better.
What V4 / the system already do
Principle	Current state
Credibility / expertise	Author YAML has bio_80w, domains, tones, metadata (background, current job, specific_struggles, strengths). That’s enough to describe a credible, consistent author.
Author as a person	One author per book: author_id in plan/manifest, chosen from persona’s pool. Same author drives cover, voice, and (where used) POV.
Name and “expertise” in intros	A0: “This book was written by {author_display_name}”. A1: one generic line, {author['display_name']} brings expertise to help you navigate {topic} with both wisdom and compassion. So the listener hears who the author is and that they “bring expertise,” but nothing more specific.
Stories and examples	Story injection is persona-aware; story bank is written using author metadata (e.g. docs/CONTENT_TEAM_STORY_WRITING_SPEC.md, docs/AUTHOR_METADATA_STORY_WRITING_GUIDE.md). So the system is set up to use author-consistent, relatable detail in stories.
Voice / narrator	Author’s voice_profile_id and narrator are used for TTS and credits, so the “voice” is tied to the author.
Belief / authenticity	HUMAN_VALIDATION.md and voice-pack docs stress “does this sound like someone who actually believes this?” and avoiding “sounds AI” — i.e. the goal is there, but it’s a content/quality bar, not a structural one.
So: we do address “who the author is” in a basic way (name, one expertise line, voice, cover, POV when used), but we don’t yet fully use the author data we have to build trust in the way the self-help advice suggests.
Gaps relative to the self-help principles
Credentials and backstory never surface in the audiobook
bio_80w, domains, and metadata (e.g. “First-gen college grad, CUNY Communications”, “Marketing coordinator at tech startup”) exist in author YAML but are not used in A0 or A1. The listener only gets “written by [Name]” and “brings expertise … wisdom and compassion.” So we don’t yet “build believable, consistent author backstories” in the listener-facing product.
One generic “author voice” line for everyone
The same sentence is used for every author (only display_name and topic change). We don’t yet “use clear, human-sounding voice and personality” or “relevant life experience” in the intro — no first-person hint, no “why I care about this,” no domain-specific line (e.g. peer vs clinician vs educator).
Author vs persona in A1 context
In intro_conclusion_builder.py, the A1 template context uses the persona’s bio_80w and struggles (audience), not the author’s bio/credentials. So the intro is built around “who this is for,” not “who is speaking and why they’re qualified.”
No AI transparency
There’s no listener-facing disclaimer or “curated for your benefit” / “AI-assisted” note in A0, A1, or metadata. So “thoughtful about AI transparency” isn’t implemented in the product yet.
Author and body prose
V4 assembly is persona/topic-driven (and story injection is persona-aware). It’s not clear that author_id shapes section choice or framing so the body feels like one consistent author’s voice. So “authentic personal voice” and “consistent in tone and values” may be only partly addressed.
What to do better (concrete)
A. Use author backstory in intros
In A1 (and optionally A0), pull from the author YAML: e.g. bio_80w or a short credential line (2–3 sentences).
Don’t just say “written by X” and “brings expertise”; say why they’re qualified (theoretical credentials, relevant experience, specialty) in natural language.
B. Author-specific “voice” line in A1
Replace the single generic “brings expertise…” sentence with something derived from the author: e.g. domains, tones, and a 1–2 sentence “why I’m here” or “what I care about” (could be a new field or a shortened bio_80w).
Optionally support a first-person variant for opinion authors (e.g. “When I worked with people in similar situations…”).
C. Keep and sharpen stories
Continue using author metadata for story writing (specific_struggles, location, background).
Optionally frame some stories as “from the author’s perspective” or “author’s experience” in the narration so the guide feels like a real person with experience, not just a generic narrator.
D. Transparency
Add an optional, configurable line (e.g. in A0 or in app metadata): e.g. “This work was created with AI assistance and curated for your benefit,” or similar, so we’re “thoughtful about AI transparency” without undermining trust.
E. Author-consistent voice across the book
In V4 (and any manifest → assembly path), consider: (1) passing author_id (and maybe author tones/domains) into section/story selection or framing, and (2) ensuring intro + body + conclusion feel like one person’s tone and values, not just “persona + topic.”
In short: V4 does address “who the author is” at a basic level, but it doesn’t yet turn the rich author data you already have into credibility, authentic voice, and transparency in the actual listener experience. The biggest levers are: surface author credentials and a personalized “why you can trust me” in A0/A1, replace the single generic author line with author-derived copy, and add optional AI transparency and, if needed, author-aware prose framing so the whole book feels like one believable, trustworthy guide.

Short answer: V4 is structurally sound, but under-expressive. You’ve built the skeleton of a world-class author system; now you need to give it muscle and voice.
Below is the smart author strategy I’d recommend — grounded in what you already have, not a rewrite fantasy.

1. First, the big picture: what authors are for in your system
Your system is correct about one thing most AI publishers miss:
Authors are the primary identity surface — not books, not personas, not topics.
An author in your system already unifies:
Content voice (domains, tones, POV)
Narration voice (voice_profile_id, narrator)
Cover identity (ACGS strategy + background pools)

Long-term brand consistency

3. Smart author strategy: 5 design principles
Principle 1: Authors should feel chosen, not assigned
Even if they’re generated.
Listeners don’t need to know how authors were created — but they do need to feel:
“This person is exactly who I’d want guiding me through this.”
Action
Lock one author per series (already mostly true)
Ensure series topics align tightly with author domains
Never rotate authors mid-series
This builds parasocial continuity.

Principle 2: Credibility must surface early — and specifically
Self-help readers decide whether to trust an author in the first 60–90 seconds.
Right now, you’re wasting that window.
What to do Replace the generic A1 author line with author-derived credibility framing.
Example pattern (third person, safe, believable):
“Alex Rivera has spent over a decade working with people navigating anxiety and burnout, blending practical tools with a grounded, human approach.”
Or for clinical:
“Dr. Sarah Johnson is a licensed clinician who has supported hundreds of patients through anxiety and recovery in high-pressure healthcare environments.”
This should be:
Generated from bio_80w + domains
1–2 sentences
Calm, non-hyped, non-credential-flexy
Key rule: Credibility ≠ credentials list Credibility = relevance + lived proximity

Principle 3: Author voice must be audible, not just stylistic
You already do style (tones, POV). What’s missing is presence.
Self-help listeners want to feel:
“Someone is here with me.”
Lightweight fix (no rewrite):
Allow occasional author-perspective framing in body prose:
“Many people I’ve worked with…”
“What I often see in moments like this…”
This does not require first-person memoirs.
It works even in third-person POV if framed as experience-based insight.
This turns advice into relational guidance.

Principle 4: Stories must feel like this author’s stories
You’re already author-aware in story writing in theory.
Make it explicit in practice:
Story bank selection should prefer:
contexts matching author domains
scenarios consistent with author background (peer vs clinician vs educator)
Avoid “omniscient advice voice” that could belong to anyone
If two authors wrote the same topic, the stories should feel different.
That’s how authors become real.

Principle 5: Transparency should be calm, not defensive
You’re right to worry about AI trust — but over-disclaiming kills authority.
Best approach
One optional, standardized line (configurable by imprint), e.g.:
“This audiobook was created with AI assistance and carefully curated to support your learning and well-being.”
Place it:
At the end of A0, or
In metadata / about section
Do not:
Apologize
Over-explain
Frame it as experimental
Calm transparency builds trust. Anxiety erodes it.

Consistent phrasing

ChatGPT said:
Perfect — here’s a clean, production-ready answer focused exactly on the two things you called out:
1) New A1 author templates 2) Author-derived credibility rules
This is written so it can be dropped straight into V4 without destabilizing anything you already built.

1. New A1 Author Templates (Listener-Facing, Trust-Optimized)
What A1 is for (re-stated precisely)
A1 is not marketing copy. A1 is the listener’s answer to:
“Why should I trust the voice guiding me through this?”
So A1 must:
Surface credibility
Sound human
Be specific but modest
Avoid hype, guarantees, or authority theater

A1 STRUCTURE (LOCKED)
A1 = 3 short parts, in this order:
Who this book is for (persona-centric)
Who the author is & why they’re relevant (author-centric)
How the author approaches this topic (values + tone)
Each part is 1 sentence. Total length: 3–5 sentences max.

A1 TEMPLATE — BASE (THIRD PERSON, SAFE DEFAULT)


This audiobook is for people navigating {persona_context}, especially when {common_struggle} feels overwhelming.

It was written by {author_display_name}, whose work focuses on {author_domain_focus}, shaped by years of experience with {relevant_context_or_population}.

Their approach is grounded, practical, and focused on helping listeners move forward with clarity rather than pressure.

Example (Healthcare RN – Burnout)
This audiobook is for nurses navigating emotional exhaustion and chronic stress, especially when caring for others starts to feel unsustainable. It was written by Sarah Johnson, a clinician whose work focuses on burnout and recovery in high-pressure healthcare environments. Her approach is steady and compassionate, emphasizing small, realistic shifts that support long-term resilience.

A1 TEMPLATE — PEER / OPINION AUTHOR (LIGHT FIRST-PERSON FLAVOR)
Use only for authors with:
pov: first_person
category: peer_, opinion_, wellness_, education_


This audiobook is for people navigating {persona_context}, especially during moments of doubt or uncertainty.

{author_display_name} has spent years working alongside people facing similar challenges, and brings that lived perspective into the way this topic is explored.

The guidance here is practical and human, shaped by real situations rather than abstract theory.

Example (Gen Z Anxiety – Peer Author)
This audiobook is for young adults navigating anxiety and self-pressure, especially when it feels hard to slow your thoughts down. Maya Chen has spent years working alongside people facing similar challenges, and brings that lived perspective into the way this topic is explored. The guidance here is practical and human, shaped by real situations rather than abstract theory.

A1 TEMPLATE — CLINICAL / EXPERT (NON-FLASHY AUTHORITY)
Use for:
clinical_
medical_
scientific_
expert_


This audiobook is for listeners seeking a clear, grounded understanding of {topic} in everyday life.

It was written by {author_display_name}, whose professional background includes work in {author_domain_focus} with a focus on {specific_application}.

The material is presented in an accessible way, balancing evidence-based insight with practical support.

Example (Clinical Anxiety)
This audiobook is for listeners seeking a clear, grounded understanding of anxiety in everyday life. It was written by Dr. Joshua Garcia, whose professional background includes clinical work in anxiety and trauma-informed care. The material is presented in an accessible way, balancing evidence-based insight with practical support.

What we explicitly avoid in A1
❌ “Expert”, “authority”, “renowned”, “leading” ❌ Credentials lists ❌ Years-count bragging ❌ Guarantees or outcomes ❌ “This book will change your life”
Calm competence > loud authority.

2. Author-Derived Credibility Rules (SYSTEM LOGIC)
This is the ruleset that decides what gets said about an author.
No free writing. No vibes.

A. Credibility Source Hierarchy (IMPORTANT)
When generating A1 author text, pull in this order:
author.domains
author.bio_80w
author.background / current_role (if present)
author.constraints (to avoid unsafe claims)
Never invent credentials. Never exaggerate.

B. Credibility Mapping Rules
Rule 1 — Match authority to author type
Author prefix
Allowed credibility framing
clinical_, medical_
“clinical work”, “professional background”, “evidence-based”
education_
“teaching”, “working with students/educators”
peer_
“working alongside”, “lived experience”, “shared challenges”
wellness_
“supporting people”, “holistic approach”
opinion_
“perspective”, “reflection”, “guidance”
expert_
“advisory work”, “strategic experience”
❌ Never say “licensed” unless explicitly encoded ❌ Never say “treats” or “diagnoses”

Rule 2 — Domain relevance check (HARD GATE)
Author domains must overlap book topic.
Example:
domains: [anxiety, burnout]
Topic: grief → ❌ FAIL
This prevents “generic expert syndrome”.

Rule 3 — No fictional life stories
Allowed:
“has worked with…”
“focuses on…”
“brings experience from…”
Not allowed:
“After losing my mother…”
“When I was diagnosed…” (unless explicitly encoded & approved)

Rule 4 — POV consistency
If pov = second_person:
A1 author credibility stays third-person
If pov = first_person:
May include one soft first-person hint
“I’ve seen…”
“I’ve worked with…”
Never mix POVs.

Rule 5 — Tone alignment
Author tones must influence:
Verb choice
Sentence length
Emotional temperature
Example:
tone: calm, steady → short sentences, no exclamations
tone: analytical → neutral verbs, structured phrasing

C. Author Trust Score (OPTIONAL, FUTURE-PROOF)
You can later compute a trust score per author:
Factor
Weight
Domain relevance
High
Specificity of bio
Medium
Tone consistency
Medium
Overclaim risk
Negative
Persona fit
High
This lets you:
Prefer high-trust authors for flagship series
Detect weak author definitions early

3. How This Improves Trust (Directly answering your concern)
Principle from self-help research
V4 after changes

Specific, domain-matched, calm
Authentic voice
Author-consistent tone + POV
Stories & examples
Already supported, now aligned

You are no longer relying on:
“Trust us, this sounds good.”
You’re structurally earning trust.

Bottom line
You don’t need fewer authors. You don’t need “real people.”
You need:
Specificity

These templates and rules do exactly that — without breaking V4.

3. Author Evaluation Dimensions (Scored)
Each author is evaluated across five axes, all of which matter for self-help trust.
3.1 Credibility Signal (0–2)
What to inspect
bio_80w
domains
background / experience metadata
title prefixes (dr_, clinical_, expert_, peer_, etc.)
Scoring
2 = clear, believable expertise OR lived experience
1 = vague expertise (“coach”, “guide”)
0 = no grounding, generic identity
Examples
✅ clinical_dr_riley_childpsych → 2
⚠️ opinion_maya_chen → 1
❌ peer_generic_helper with no experience → 0

3.2 Voice Authenticity (0–2)
What to inspect
POV (first vs third)
tone metadata
category prefix (peer_, opinion_, leadership_, clinical_)
existing intro copy (if present)
Scoring
2 = sounds like a real person Gen Z/Alpha could meet
1 = acceptable but generic
0 = institutional / academic / synthetic
Red flags
Over-formal language
Abstract authority without lived detail
“Textbook narrator” vibes

3.3 Persona Alignment (0–2)
What to inspect
Which persona pools the author appears in
Whether their background matches the persona psychologically
Scoring
2 = clean alignment (e.g. peer Gen Z → Gen Z anxiety)
1 = usable but mismatched tone
0 = persona violation
Example
❌ corporate_strategy_expert in Gen Alpha anxiety pool → 0

3.4 Story Readiness (0–2)
What to inspect
Presence of:
lived experience markers
specific struggles
location / age / context hooks
Ability to generate credible anecdotes
Scoring
2 = story-rich
1 = needs templated case studies
0 = cannot support narrative self-help
This is critical: self-help without stories = no trust.

3.5 Transparency Compatibility (0–1)
What to inspect
Is the author positioned as:
human guide
curated voice
fictional persona
Does their profile allow safe AI-assisted disclosure?
Scoring
1 = compatible with AI-assisted transparency
0 = misleading authority risk

4️⃣ PLANNER HARD CONSTRAINTS (NEW VALIDATORS)
📄 phoenix_v4/planner/corp_niche_constraints.py
A. Corp Isolation Validator


def validate_corp_isolation(plan):
    assert plan.corp_id is not None
    assert plan.author.corp_id == plan.corp_id
    assert plan.narrator.corp_id == plan.corp_id


B. Audience Ratio Validator


def validate_audience_ratio(plan, corp):
    youth = plan.stats.youth_titles_ratio
    if not (corp.youth_ratio.min <= youth <= corp.youth_ratio.max):
        raise AudienceRatioViolationError


C. Author Eligibility Validator


def validate_author_fit(author):
    assert author.strategy_fit in ["STRONG_FIT", "USABLE_WITH_AUGMENTATION"]


D. Generation Safety Validator


def validate_generation_safety(author, persona):
    if persona == "gen_alpha_students":
        assert author.prefix not in [
            "leadership_", "operations_", "expert_",
            "scientific_", "specialized_"
        ]

You said:
what's the best way to plan series and book titles: Here’s how Phoenix Raising names dose series and books, and where the title engine(s) fit in.
Two title systems
There are two separate “title engine” layers:
Chapter / in-book title engine — cluster_therapy_tok/core/title_engine.py
Used when building a book/session: chapter titles and theme summaries.
No series or “dose” concept; it’s per-chapter, per-book.
Book/series title engine — tools/title_engine.py
Used for batch/CSV and series workflows: book titles, subtitles, and series names (including “dose” series).
Persona/topic-aware (Gen Z, Gen Alpha, Hybrid), with series position (Book 1, 2, …).
Dose series naming (Phoenix Raising)
“Dose series” = one series = one persona + one format, with one book per topic (e.g. anxiety, stress, sleep).
Series name is set in the Desktop app before calling the series builder:
series_name = f"{persona.replace('_', ' ').title()} — {fmt.replace('_', ' ')}"
Example: "Gen Z — standard book", "Gen Alpha — cluster_session".
That series_name is passed into build_phoenix_series_package() in tools/phoenix_series_builder.py.
Inside the series builder, per-book titles are set without the book/series title engine:
Book title:
f"{_pretty(topic)} — {_pretty(persona)} (Book {i})"
e.g. "Anxiety — Gen Z (Book 1)".
Book subtitle:
f"{series_name} • Book {i} of {len(topics)}"
e.g. "Gen Z — standard book • Book 1 of 5".
So dose series naming is: persona + format for the series, and topic + persona + position for each book, all done in the series builder, not by tools/title_engine.py.
Book naming when building a single book/session
When the orchestrator builds one book (e.g. from the Desktop app or batch):
If book_title is provided (e.g. from series builder or CSV): that value is used for the manifest and A0/A1.
If book_title is missing and the chapter title engine is available:
It calls generate_title_and_summary(...) from cluster_therapy_tok.core.title_engine.
That function is chapter-oriented (topic, beat_map, book_arc_stage, chapter_texts, persona). The orchestrator uses it to get a title for the whole book when none was passed (e.g. single-book runs). So the “book title” in that path is effectively one generated chapter-style title used as the book title.
Chapter titles inside the book are generated by the same module:
generate_title_and_summary(topic=..., beat_map=..., book_arc_stage=..., chapter_texts=..., persona=..., style="poetic")
returns title and theme_summary per chapter.
Vocab comes from SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/vocab/: persona_vocabulary.yaml, theme_keywords.yaml, emotion_gradients.yaml.
Patterns are things like “{Arc modifier} {theme}”, “The {tone} {modifier}”, etc., with arc stages (Awakening, Transformation, Integration, etc.).
So: book-level naming when no title is provided uses the chapter title engine once; chapter-level naming uses it per chapter. Neither uses tools/title_engine.py in the current Phoenix Raising series flow.
Where the book/series title engine is used
tools/title_engine.py is used for:
Batch/CSV title generation — tools/batch_title_generator.py, tools/generate_10k_books_config.py (optional --generate-titles).
108-series style generation — tools/generate_108_series.py uses generate_series_titles() from tools/title_engine.py to get series + per-book titles.
That engine provides:
Series name: e.g. generate_series_name(series_type) → “The Anxiety Protocol”, “The Anxiety Era Series”, “The Anxiety Solutions”, etc.
Series subtitle per book: e.g. “Book 1 of {series_name}”, “Part 2: Going Deeper”, “Volume 3: Integration” (persona-dependent).
Position-aware book titles: Book 1 = foundation, 2 = going deeper, 3 = integration, middle = transformation, final = mastery, with persona-specific hooks (Gen Z / Gen Alpha / Hybrid).
So: dose series and book naming in the main Phoenix Raising path (Desktop → series builder → orchestrator) do not go through this engine; they use the series builder’s template and, when needed, the chapter title engine. The book/series title engine is used for batch and 108-series workflows and could be wired into Phoenix Raising later if you want persona/series-position titles there too.

I’ll answer this in four layers:
The correct mental model (what each title layer is responsible for)
The recommended V4 planning hierarchy (what gets decided when)

This keeps Phoenix Raising strong and makes 108-book / 1000-book expansion safe.

1️⃣ The correct mental model (this is the key unlock)
You don’t actually have “two title engines.”
You have three different semantic layers of naming, and each one serves a different business purpose:
Layer
Purpose
Audience
Engine
Brand / Series naming
Shelf positioning, discoverability, trust
Storefronts, algorithms
tools/title_engine.py
Book naming
Conversion + expectation setting
Buyer / listener
series builder OR title engine
Chapter naming
Emotional flow + retention
Listener
cluster_therapy_tok/core/title_engine.py
The confusion only happens if these layers bleed into each other. Right now, they’re mostly separated — which is good.

2️⃣ The correct V4 planning hierarchy (THIS is the “best way”)
In V4, titles should be planned top-down, not generated opportunistically.
🔒 V4 Golden Rule
Series names are a strategic decision. Book titles are a structural decision. Chapter titles are an experiential decision.
That gives us this order:

🧭 V4 Planning Order (authoritative)
1. Corp → Niche → Brand
Defined once. Never generated ad hoc.
Example:
Corp: Anxiety Shift System
Brand promise: “Rewire the anxious brain”
Shelf identity: recognizable, repeatable
This must not come from chapter logic.

2. Series definition (human-set or batch-generated)
A series answers:
Who is this for?
In what format?
Over how many books?
Example:


series_id: anxiety_shift_gen_z_standard
series_name: "Anxiety Shift System — Gen Z Edition"
series_format: standard_book
persona: gen_z

✅ This is where tools/title_engine.py belongs.
It’s the only engine that understands:
Series positioning
Persona-aware progression
Book order psychology

3. Book titles (position-aware, deterministic)
Books should never be named “on the fly” in production-scale systems.
Best practice:
Titles are either:
generated once in planning (CSV / batch / 108-series)
or templated deterministically in the series builder
Phoenix Raising currently does the templated version:


Anxiety — Gen Z (Book 1)

That is:
Safe
Clear
Algorithm-friendly
Slightly unsexy, but very scalable
That’s okay — especially for “dose” series.

4. Chapter titles (emotional + poetic)
This is where creativity belongs.
Your current setup here is excellent:
Persona vocab
Arc stages
Emotional gradients
Poetic / reflective patterns
And crucially:
Chapter titles do not affect store metadata.
So they can be expressive without risk.

3️⃣ Clear rules for series vs book vs chapter titles
Here’s the V4 rule-set I recommend locking in.

✅ Rule A — Series names are brand assets
Set by humans or batch planners
Stored in corp / series YAML
Never regenerated per run
✔ tools/title_engine.py ✖ cluster_therapy_tok

✅ Rule B — Book titles must be predictable
Choose one of these per workflow:
Option 1: Dose / curriculum series (Phoenix Raising default)
Use template-based titles:


{Topic} — {Persona} (Book {N})

Why:
Clear expectation
Fast localization
Zero duplication risk
Perfect for Spotify / Google Play scale
This is what you already do — and it’s correct.

Option 2: Premium / 108-book series
Use tools/title_engine.py:
Position-aware hooks
Foundation → mastery progression
Persona-specific language
Example:
Book 1: The Anxiety Reset
Book 6: Staying Calm When It Matters
Book 12: Mastering Emotional Stability
These should be generated once, saved, and reused.

❌ Rule C — Chapter engine should not name series
Right now this only happens when:
book_title is missing
single-book runs occur
That’s acceptable only as a fallback.
But:
Chapter title engine output should never be treated as a canonical book title for series publishing.

4️⃣ Concrete V4 recommendations (actionable)
✅ Keep exactly as-is
cluster_therapy_tok/core/title_engine.py for chapters
Phoenix Raising dose naming for rapid series
Passing explicit book_title into orchestrator
These are solid.

only when --use-series-title-engine

ChatGPT said:
You’re right to question this — {Topic} — {Persona} (Book N) is safe, but it is not optimal for SEO or conversion on Google Play, Apple Books, or Spotify when you’re running premium / 108-book series.
Below is the clear, platform-aware answer and the best-practice structure you should lock into V4.

🎯 The Core Truth About SEO for Audiobooks (2025–2026 reality)
Across Google Play, Apple Books, Spotify, Audible-style systems, the ranking stack looks like this (in order of impact):
🔑 What actually matters for discovery
Title keywords (highest weight)
Subtitle keywords (very high weight)
Series name (high, especially for internal recs)
Description opening paragraph
Author name consistency
Categories / BISAC / genre tags
Engagement signals (completion, saves)
👉 Persona words (“Gen Z”, “for Teens”, “for Students”) matter far more than Book numbers. 👉 Book numbers matter for series cohesion, not SEO.
So your instinct is correct.

❌ Why {Topic} — {Persona} (Book N) is weak marketing
Problems:
Sounds academic / internal
Wastes the title on metadata
Low emotional pull
Persona terms are better contextualized, not blunt
It’s okay for internal systems, but not for storefronts.

✅ The SEO-OPTIMAL STRUCTURE (this is the answer you want)
🏆 Best Overall Pattern (Works Everywhere)
TITLE
Outcome-Driven + Emotional + Topic-Anchored
SUBTITLE
Persona + Situation + Series Position (optional)
SERIES
Brand / Protocol / System Name

🔥 Concrete Example (Anxiety, Gen Z, Premium Series)
❌ Old (bad marketing)


Anxiety — Gen Z (Book 3)

✅ New (SEO + conversion optimized)
Title:


Calm Under Pressure

Subtitle:


An Anxiety Reset for Gen Z Students • Book 3 of the Anxiety Shift System

Series:


Anxiety Shift System

Why this wins:
“Calm” + “Pressure” = high-intent search terms
“Anxiety” is still present (subtitle)
“Gen Z Students” targets persona searches
“Book 3” is demoted to structural info (correct)

📊 Platform-Specific SEO Guidance
🟢 Google Play Books (MOST IMPORTANT FOR YOU)
Google Play behaves like Google Search.
Best practices:
Title: emotional + benefit-oriented (5–7 words)
Subtitle: include:
topic keyword
persona
situational phrase (“for students”, “for work”, etc.)
Series name is indexed and strongly boosts recs
💡 Google often shows subtitle text in search snippets.

🍎 Apple Books
Apple favors:
Clean titles
Clear subtitles
Strong series branding
Apple penalizes keyword stuffing, so:
Do not put “Gen Z” + “Teens” + “Students” + “Young Adults” all in the title
One persona term is enough

🎧 Spotify / Subscription Audio
Spotify discovery is:
Topic clusters
Completion rate
Series continuation
Titles should:
Be emotionally legible
Avoid academic phrasing
Sound like something a human would tap
Example:


Finding Calm When Everything Feels Too Much

Location is injected only if:
Persona has location_overlay != null
Location is meaningful for that persona (students, workers, families)
🧠 Format


in {City}
in {Region}
in {Country}

Never:
Zip codes
Over-specific neighborhoods
Multiple locations
This keeps Apple + Google happy and avoids spam flags.

This is exactly right — series subtitles carry the SEO weight, not book titles.

Pattern

Is indexed once
Applies to all 108 books

    - persona
    - location

  template: >
    {topic_phrase} for {persona_label}{location_clause}


📄 Location Clause Logic (planner)


def location_clause(persona):
    if persona.location_overlay:
        return f" in {persona.location_overlay}"
    return ""

ChatGPT said:
Yes — and you’re exactly right to worry about this. What you’re sensing is the platform-pattern detection problem. If 24 brands use the same linguistic skeleton, stores and rec systems eventually treat them as one factory.
The good news: you do not need chaos to get uniqueness. You just need controlled variation.
Below is the clean, V4-safe way to introduce brand-level uniqueness without breaking SEO, scalability, or planner determinism.

🎯 THE CORE IDEA (LOCK THIS IN)
One global structure, multiple brand-specific “expression lenses.”
Meaning:
The semantic structure stays the same (SEO-safe)
The surface language varies per brand (pattern-safe)
Platforms see:
Consistent metadata quality
But distinct editorial voices per brand

🧱 THE 4 PLACES YOU CAN ADD UNIQUENESS (WITHOUT RISK)
There are exactly four safe injection points. You do not need to vary everything.

1️⃣ Series Subtitle Style (PRIMARY DIFFERENTIATOR)
Instead of one subtitle template, each brand gets a subtitle style family.
🔒 Still SEO-safe
Persona
Topic cluster
Self-help framing
🎨 But linguistically distinct per brand

Example: SAME NICHE (Anxiety), DIFFERENT BRANDS
Brand A — Anxiety Shift System (practical, grounded)


Practical Self-Help Tools for Gen Z Students Navigating Anxiety, Stress, and Overthinking

Brand B — Inner Peace Protocol (calm, reflective)


Gentle Audio Practices for Students Seeking Calm, Balance, and Emotional Stability

Brand C — CalmBody Toolkit (somatic, embodied)


Body-Based Tools for Young People Regulating Anxiety, Tension, and Emotional Overload

Brand D — Confidence DNA (empowering, energetic)


Confidence-Building Audio for Students Overcoming Anxiety and Self-Doubt

Same topics. Different editorial DNA.
📌 This is the most important uniqueness lever.

2️⃣ Subtitle Micro-Clause (SECONDARY, OPTIONAL)
You can add one short brand signature clause at the end of the book subtitle.
Pattern


{Topic} for {Persona}{Location Clause} — {Brand Micro-Signature}

Examples
— A Calm, Practical Audio Guide
— Tools You Can Use Right Now
— Designed for Real Life, Not Perfection
— Grounded Support for Modern Life
🔑 Rules:
Max 5–7 words
No keywords
No repetition across brands
Optional per brand (not per book)
This creates subtle differentiation without SEO noise.

3️⃣ Outcome Vocabulary Bank (TITLE LEVEL)
Titles stay outcome-driven, but each brand draws from a different outcome vocabulary bank.
Same structure, different emotional tone
Brand Outcome Styles
Brand Type
Outcome Vocabulary
Calm / Somatic
calm, grounded, settled, steady
Empowerment
confident, strong, capable, resilient
Reflective
clarity, center, alignment, presence
Action-Oriented
focus, momentum, direction, traction
Creative
flow, expression, spark, voice

Example: Same Topic, Different Brands
Calm Under Pressure (somatic brand)
Staying Steady When Life Hits (reflective brand)
Confidence When It Counts (empowerment brand)
Focus When Everything Competes (performance brand)
Platforms see different phrasing patterns, not clones.

4️⃣ Description Opening Sentence (HIGH IMPACT, LOW RISK)
Even if everything else matched, this alone breaks pattern detection.
Each brand gets one opening sentence style for descriptions.
Examples
Practical brand: “This audiobook offers clear, practical tools you can use immediately…”
Compassionate brand: “If you’ve been feeling overwhelmed or stretched thin, this audiobook is here to support you…”
Empowerment brand: “You don’t need to fix yourself — you need tools that actually work…”
Reflective brand: “In a world that constantly pulls at your attention, this audiobook invites you to slow down…”
💡 This is outside titles, but platforms absolutely read it.

🧠 WHAT NOT TO DO (IMPORTANT)
❌ Don’t randomize templates per book ❌ Don’t stuff keywords differently per brand ❌ Don’t rotate personas in titles ❌ Don’t invent wildly different structures
That does look like spam.
We want editorial coherence, not randomness.

🧬 THE V4 WAY TO IMPLEMENT THIS (DEV-SPEC)
📄 niche_brand_voice.yaml


brand_id: anxiety_shift_system

series_subtitle_style: practical
subtitle_micro_signature: "Tools You Can Use Right Now"

  - calm

  - capable

description_opening_style: practical_clear

Another brand:


brand_id: inner_peace_protocol

series_subtitle_style: gentle_reflective
subtitle_micro_signature: "A Calm, Supportive Audio Guide"

title_outcome_bank:
  - calm
  - balance
  - presence
  - ease

description_opening_style: compassionate

Planner selects one style per brand, never per book.

🧩 WHY THIS WORKS (AND SCALES TO 1000+ BOOKS)
✔ Platforms see distinct editorial brands ✔ SEO remains consistent and strong ✔ Series feel intentional, not templated ✔ Authors and narrators still feel human ✔ You can add more brands without refactoring
This is how real publishers avoid being flagged while still automating.

✅ FINAL RECOMMENDATION (CRISP)
Do not vary structure. Do not vary rules per book.
👉 Vary expression per brand across:
Series subtitle style
Outcome vocabulary
Optional subtitle micro-signature
Description opening sentence
That’s the sweet spot between:
scale
safety
brand identity
algorithm trust

PART III — BRAND-AWARE TITLE GENERATOR (DEV SPEC)
📄 tools/brand_aware_title_generator.py


def generate_book_title(topic, brand_profile):
    outcome = pick_from(brand_profile.outcome_vocab)
    pattern = pick_from([
        f"{outcome.title()} Under Pressure",
        f"Finding {outcome.title()}",
        f"{outcome.title()} When It Matters",
        f"Staying {outcome.title()} in Real Life"
    ])
    return pattern

Subtitle generator:


def generate_book_subtitle(topic, persona, location, brand_profile):
    base = f"{topic.phrase} for {persona.label}"
    if location:
        base += f" in {location}"
    if brand_profile.micro_signature:
        base += f" — {brand_profile.micro_signature}"
    return base

✔ Platforms see 24 distinct editorial brands ✔ SEO structure remains identical (safe) ✔ Language varies enough to avoid clustering ✔ Authors and narrators still feel human ✔ You can add new brands without refactoring logic
This is exactly how scaled publishers avoid suppression while automating.

Current state





Series/book titles (Phoenix Raising): tools/phoenix_series_builder.py sets book_title = f"{Topic} — {Persona} (Book {i})" and book_subtitle = f"{series_name} • Book {i} of {len(topics)}" (lines 304–305). No series subtitle; series name is from Desktop app: {Persona} — {Format}.



Title engine (book/series): tools/title_engine.py has TitleEngine, location-aware subtitles, series position patterns, and LOCATION_MAPPINGS for three personas (SF, LA). Used by batch/108-series, not by Phoenix series builder.

Architecture (three layers, no bleed)

flowchart LR
  subgraph brand [Brand / Series]
    Plan[series_title_plan.yaml]
    BrandVoice[niche_brand_voice.yaml]
  end
  subgraph book [Book]
    BookTitle[Outcome title 3-7 words]
    BookSub[Topic for Persona + location + micro]
  end
  subgraph chapter [Chapter]
    ChapterEngine[cluster_therapy_tok title_engine]
  end
  Plan --> BookTitle
  Plan --> BookSub
  BrandVoice --> BookTitle
  BrandVoice --> BookSub
  ChapterEngine -.->|never| Plan





Series: Name + series subtitle (persona + topic cluster, style from brand). Stored in plan; reused forever.



Book: Title = outcome-driven (brand outcome bank). Subtitle = topic + persona + optional location + optional micro-signature. No series position in subtitle.



Chapter: Existing cluster_therapy_tok title_engine only; no change.

1. Artifacts and schemas

1.1 Series Title Plan

Path: artifacts/series_title_plans/{series_id}.yaml (or config/series_title_plans/ if you prefer config over artifacts).

Schema (minimal):

series_id: anxiety_shift_gen_z_students_ny
series_name: "Anxiety Shift System"
brand_id: anxiety_shift_system   # optional; links to niche_brand_voice

series_subtitle:
  template: "Practical Self-Help Tools for {persona_label} Navigating {topic_cluster}"
  # topic_cluster = comma-separated from series topics, e.g. "Anxiety, Stress, and Overthinking"

book_title_rules:
  type: outcome_driven
  min_words: 3
  max_words: 7
  forbid: [persona, location, series_position]

book_subtitle_rules:
  template: "{topic_phrase} for {persona_label}{location_clause}"
  # location_clause = " in {location}" only if persona has location overlay
  # Optional micro_signature from brand appended: " — {subtitle_micro_signature}"

# Optional: pre-generated per-book overrides (for 108-series or editorial)
books:
  1: { title: "Calm Under Pressure", subtitle: "An Anxiety Reset for Gen Z Students in New York" }
  2: { title: "Staying Steady When Life Hits", subtitle: "A Stress Guide for Gen Z Students in New York" }





If books is present for a position, use it; otherwise generate from rules + brand voice.



topic_phrase = human-readable topic (e.g. "An Anxiety Reset", "A Stress Guide") — can be a small topic→phrase map or derived from topic.

1.2 Niche Brand Voice

Path: config/niche_brand_voice/{brand_id}.yaml (or artifacts/niche_brand_voice/).

Schema:

brand_id: anxiety_shift_system

series_subtitle_style: practical   # practical | gentle_reflective | somatic | empowerment
subtitle_micro_signature: "Tools You Can Use Right Now"   # optional, 5-7 words

title_outcome_bank:
  - calm
  - steady
  - grounded
  - capable

description_opening_style: practical_clear   # optional; for first sentence of series description





series_subtitle_style: Drives which series-subtitle template family to use (same variables: persona_label, topic_cluster). Different styles = different surface phrasing to avoid platform pattern detection.



title_outcome_bank: Words used to build outcome-driven book titles (3–7 words, no persona/location).



subtitle_micro_signature: Appended to book subtitle when present; optional per brand.



description_opening_style: Used when generating series description (first sentence); can be implemented in a follow-up.

2. Location for subtitle

Rule: Location appears only in book subtitle, as " in {City}" or " in {Region}", and only when the target persona has a location.

Implementation options:





A (recommended): Derive once in a small shared module used by title and series builder:





If persona is in title_engine.LOCATION_MAPPINGS, use city or region for display.



Else if unified_personas.personas[persona_id].location_data.location exists, use that value title-cased (e.g. atlanta → "Atlanta", boston → "Boston") and optionally map known slugs to "New York", "San Francisco", etc.



B: Add optional location_overlay (display string) to each persona in unified_personas and use that when present.

Output: location_clause = "" or " in {display_location}"; no zip codes or multiple locations.

3. Title generation logic

3.1 Outcome-driven book title (3–7 words)





Inputs: topic, brand outcome bank (from niche_brand_voice), optional seed for determinism.



Logic: Combine outcome words + topic/context into short phrases (e.g. "Calm Under Pressure", "Staying Steady When Life Hits"). Can be template-based (e.g. "{outcome} Under {pressure}", "{outcome} When {situation}") with words from bank; no persona/location/series position.



Placement: New helper in tools/title_engine.py or a dedicated tools/v4_title_planner.py that both the series builder and 108-series can call. Prefer extending title_engine so one place owns all book/series title rules.

3.2 Book subtitle





Formula: {topic_phrase} for {persona_label}{location_clause} + optionally  — {subtitle_micro_signature}.



persona_label: From unified_personas.personas[persona_id].label or fallback to persona_id.replace("_", " ").title().



topic_phrase: From a small topic→phrase map (e.g. anxiety → "An Anxiety Reset", stress → "A Stress Guide") or one-line derivation.



location_clause: From step 2.



No series position or book number.

3.3 Series subtitle





Formula: Template from plan (e.g. "Practical Self-Help Tools for {persona_label} Navigating {topic_cluster}").



topic_cluster: Comma-separated list of series topics (e.g. "Anxiety, Stress, and Overthinking"); built from series topics.



Style (brand): Map series_subtitle_style to one of several template strings so the same variables produce different phrasing per brand (practical vs gentle_reflective vs somatic vs empowerment). Store template families in code or a small YAML; brand picks style, not free text.

4. Integration points

4.1 Phoenix Series Builder (tools/phoenix_series_builder.py)





Series name: Already passed in as series_name. Optional: if a series plan exists (e.g. by series_id derived from series_name or passed explicitly), load plan and use series_name from plan; otherwise keep current behavior.



Series subtitle: If plan exists and has series_subtitle, generate series subtitle (persona_label, topic_cluster, style from brand) and persist it on the series JSON and in Google Play metadata. If no plan, leave series_subtitle blank or use a single generic line.



Per-book titles:





If plan exists and has books[position], use books[position].title and books[position].subtitle.



Else if plan exists with only rules: generate book title via outcome engine (brand outcome bank) and book subtitle via template (topic_phrase, persona_label, location_clause, micro_signature). Pass persona and topic from book config; resolve persona_label and location from unified_personas + location logic.



If no plan: keep current dose template {Topic} — {Persona} (Book {i}) and {series_name} • Book {i} of {len(topics)} so existing flows are unchanged.



Plan lookup: Either (1) require an explicit path to the plan, or (2) derive series_id from series_name (e.g. slug) and look up artifacts/series_title_plans/{series_id}.yaml. If you later add series_id as an input to the builder, use that for lookup.

4.2 Series metadata (tools/series_metadata_generator.py)





generate_series_google_play_metadata: Accept optional series_subtitle from series_metadata (e.g. from plan). If present, use it for google_play.subtitle and pass into generate_series_description so the description’s opening reflects the SEO series subtitle instead of "Complete Series (N books)".



generate_series_description: Already takes series_subtitle; ensure the generated description uses it in the first line when provided.

4.3 Optional: use title engine when no plan





Add a flag (e.g. --use-series-title-engine) to the Desktop app or series builder: when building a series without a plan, call tools/title_engine.py to generate outcome titles and subtitles (with location if persona has one), then optionally write a generated plan to artifacts/series_title_plans/ for reuse. This supports 108-series and “generate once, reuse forever” without requiring a pre-made plan.

5. Brand uniqueness (no template bleed)





Series subtitle: Multiple template families keyed by series_subtitle_style; same variables, different wording per style (practical, gentle_reflective, somatic, empowerment).



Outcome bank: Each brand has its own title_outcome_bank; outcome titles are built from that bank only.



Micro-signature: Optional short phrase per brand on book subtitle; no keywords, 5–7 words.



Description opening: description_opening_style can select different first-sentence templates for the series description (implement when touching series_metadata_generator).

6. What stays unchanged





Chapter titles: Only cluster_therapy_tok/core/title_engine.py; never used for series or book names.



Orchestrator: Continues to accept book_title and book_subtitle from the caller (series builder or single-book flow); no change to orchestrator title logic.



Single-book runs: If they pass explicit book_title/book_subtitle, behavior unchanged. If they rely on chapter engine fallback for book title, that remains a fallback only for non-series runs.

You said:
grog?; V4 Series and Book Title System

Current state





Series/book titles (Phoenix Raising): tools/phoenix_series_builder.py sets book_title = f"{Topic} — {Persona} (Book {i})" and book_subtitle = f"{series_name} • Book {i} of {len(topics)}" (lines 304–305). No series subtitle; series name is from Desktop app: {Persona} — {Format}.



Title engine (book/series): tools/title_engine.py has TitleEngine, location-aware subtitles, series position patterns, and LOCATION_MAPPINGS for three personas (SF, LA). Used by batch/108-series, not by Phoenix series builder.



Chapter titles: cluster_therapy_tok/core/title_engine.py — unchanged; remains the only source for chapter titles.



Persona/location: unified_personas.yaml has label per persona and location_data.location (e.g. atlanta, boston) for regional personas. tools/title_engine.py has hardcoded LOCATION_MAPPINGS for gen_z_san_francisco, gen_alpha_san_francisco, gen_alpha_la.



Series metadata: tools/series_metadata_generator.py builds Google Play metadata; series_subtitle is currently fixed as "Complete Series (N books)" (line 207).

Architecture (three layers, no bleed)

flowchart LR
  subgraph brand [Brand / Series]
    Plan[series_title_plan.yaml]
    BrandVoice[niche_brand_voice.yaml]
  end
  subgraph book [Book]
    BookTitle[Outcome title 3-7 words]
    BookSub[Topic for Persona + location + micro]
  end
  subgraph chapter [Chapter]
    ChapterEngine[cluster_therapy_tok title_engine]
  end
  Plan --> BookTitle
  Plan --> BookSub
  BrandVoice --> BookTitle
  BrandVoice --> BookSub
  ChapterEngine -.->|never| Plan





Series: Name + series subtitle (persona + topic cluster, style from brand). Stored in plan; reused forever.



Book: Title = outcome-driven (brand outcome bank). Subtitle = topic + persona + optional location + optional micro-signature. No series position in subtitle.



Chapter: Existing cluster_therapy_tok title_engine only; no change.

1. Artifacts and schemas

1.1 Series Title Plan

Path: artifacts/series_title_plans/{series_id}.yaml (or config/series_title_plans/ if you prefer config over artifacts).

Schema (minimal):

series_id: anxiety_shift_gen_z_students_ny
series_name: "Anxiety Shift System"
brand_id: anxiety_shift_system   # optional; links to niche_brand_voice

series_subtitle:
  template: "Practical Self-Help Tools for {persona_label} Navigating {topic_cluster}"
  # topic_cluster = comma-separated from series topics, e.g. "Anxiety, Stress, and Overthinking"

book_title_rules:
  type: outcome_driven
  min_words: 3
  max_words: 7
  forbid: [persona, location, series_position]

book_subtitle_rules:
  template: "{topic_phrase} for {persona_label}{location_clause}"
  # location_clause = " in {location}" only if persona has location overlay
  # Optional micro_signature from brand appended: " — {subtitle_micro_signature}"

# Optional: pre-generated per-book overrides (for 108-series or editorial)
books:
  1: { title: "Calm Under Pressure", subtitle: "An Anxiety Reset for Gen Z Students in New York" }
  2: { title: "Staying Steady When Life Hits", subtitle: "A Stress Guide for Gen Z Students in New York" }





If books is present for a position, use it; otherwise generate from rules + brand voice.



topic_phrase = human-readable topic (e.g. "An Anxiety Reset", "A Stress Guide") — can be a small topic→phrase map or derived from topic.

1.2 Niche Brand Voice

Path: config/niche_brand_voice/{brand_id}.yaml (or artifacts/niche_brand_voice/).

Schema:

brand_id: anxiety_shift_system

series_subtitle_style: practical   # practical | gentle_reflective | somatic | empowerment
subtitle_micro_signature: "Tools You Can Use Right Now"   # optional, 5-7 words

title_outcome_bank:
  - calm
  - steady
  - grounded
  - capable

description_opening_style: practical_clear   # optional; for first sentence of series description





series_subtitle_style: Drives which series-subtitle template family to use (same variables: persona_label, topic_cluster). Different styles = different surface phrasing to avoid platform pattern detection.



title_outcome_bank: Words used to build outcome-driven book titles (3–7 words, no persona/location).



subtitle_micro_signature: Appended to book subtitle when present; optional per brand.



description_opening_style: Used when generating series description (first sentence); can be implemented in a follow-up.

2. Location for subtitle

Rule: Location appears only in book subtitle, as " in {City}" or " in {Region}", and only when the target persona has a location.

Implementation options:





A (recommended): Derive once in a small shared module used by title and series builder:





If persona is in title_engine.LOCATION_MAPPINGS, use city or region for display.



Else if unified_personas.personas[persona_id].location_data.location exists, use that value title-cased (e.g. atlanta → "Atlanta", boston → "Boston") and optionally map known slugs to "New York", "San Francisco", etc.



B: Add optional location_overlay (display string) to each persona in unified_personas and use that when present.

Output: location_clause = "" or " in {display_location}"; no zip codes or multiple locations.

3. Title generation logic

3.1 Outcome-driven book title (3–7 words)





Inputs: topic, brand outcome bank (from niche_brand_voice), optional seed for determinism.



Logic: Combine outcome words + topic/context into short phrases (e.g. "Calm Under Pressure", "Staying Steady When Life Hits"). Can be template-based (e.g. "{outcome} Under {pressure}", "{outcome} When {situation}") with words from bank; no persona/location/series position.



Placement: New helper in tools/title_engine.py or a dedicated tools/v4_title_planner.py that both the series builder and 108-series can call. Prefer extending title_engine so one place owns all book/series title rules.

3.2 Book subtitle





Formula: {topic_phrase} for {persona_label}{location_clause} + optionally  — {subtitle_micro_signature}.



persona_label: From unified_personas.personas[persona_id].label or fallback to persona_id.replace("_", " ").title().



topic_phrase: From a small topic→phrase map (e.g. anxiety → "An Anxiety Reset", stress → "A Stress Guide") or one-line derivation.



location_clause: From step 2.



No series position or book number.

3.3 Series subtitle





Formula: Template from plan (e.g. "Practical Self-Help Tools for {persona_label} Navigating {topic_cluster}").



topic_cluster: Comma-separated list of series topics (e.g. "Anxiety, Stress, and Overthinking"); built from series topics.



Style (brand): Map series_subtitle_style to one of several template strings so the same variables produce different phrasing per brand (practical vs gentle_reflective vs somatic vs empowerment). Store template families in code or a small YAML; brand picks style, not free text.

4. Integration points

4.1 Phoenix Series Builder (tools/phoenix_series_builder.py)





Series name: Already passed in as series_name. Optional: if a series plan exists (e.g. by series_id derived from series_name or passed explicitly), load plan and use series_name from plan; otherwise keep current behavior.



Series subtitle: If plan exists and has series_subtitle, generate series subtitle (persona_label, topic_cluster, style from brand) and persist it on the series JSON and in Google Play metadata. If no plan, leave series_subtitle blank or use a single generic line.



Per-book titles:





If plan exists and has books[position], use books[position].title and books[position].subtitle.



Else if plan exists with only rules: generate book title via outcome engine (brand outcome bank) and book subtitle via template (topic_phrase, persona_label, location_clause, micro_signature). Pass persona and topic from book config; resolve persona_label and location from unified_personas + location logic.



If no plan: keep current dose template {Topic} — {Persona} (Book {i}) and {series_name} • Book {i} of {len(topics)} so existing flows are unchanged.



Plan lookup: Either (1) require an explicit path to the plan, or (2) derive series_id from series_name (e.g. slug) and look up artifacts/series_title_plans/{series_id}.yaml. If you later add series_id as an input to the builder, use that for lookup.

4.2 Series metadata (tools/series_metadata_generator.py)





generate_series_google_play_metadata: Accept optional series_subtitle from series_metadata (e.g. from plan). If present, use it for google_play.subtitle and pass into generate_series_description so the description’s opening reflects the SEO series subtitle instead of "Complete Series (N books)".



generate_series_description: Already takes series_subtitle; ensure the generated description uses it in the first line when provided.

4.3 Optional: use title engine when no plan





Add a flag (e.g. --use-series-title-engine) to the Desktop app or series builder: when building a series without a plan, call tools/title_engine.py to generate outcome titles and subtitles (with location if persona has one), then optionally write a generated plan to artifacts/series_title_plans/ for reuse. This supports 108-series and “generate once, reuse forever” without requiring a pre-made plan.

5. Brand uniqueness (no template bleed)





Series subtitle: Multiple template families keyed by series_subtitle_style; same variables, different wording per style (practical, gentle_reflective, somatic, empowerment).



Outcome bank: Each brand has its own title_outcome_bank; outcome titles are built from that bank only.



Micro-signature: Optional short phrase per brand on book subtitle; no keywords, 5–7 words.



Description opening: description_opening_style can select different first-sentence templates for the series description (implement when touching series_metadata_generator).

6. What stays unchanged





Chapter titles: Only cluster_therapy_tok/core/title_engine.py; never used for series or book names.



Orchestrator: Continues to accept book_title and book_subtitle from the caller (series builder or single-book flow); no change to orchestrator title logic.



Single-book runs: If they pass explicit book_title/book_subtitle, behavior unchanged. If they rely on chapter engine fallback for book title, that remains a fallback only for non-series runs.

7. File and dependency summary







Item



Action





artifacts/series_title_plans/ (or config/series_title_plans/)



New; store per-series YAML plans





config/niche_brand_voice/ (or artifacts/niche_brand_voice/)



New; store per-brand YAML voice

Add: outcome title generator (uses outcome bank), book subtitle builder (topic_phrase, persona_label, location_clause, micro_signature), series subtitle generator (template + style). Extend or replace location source with unified_personas-based resolver.





Shared location resolver



New helper (in title_engine or small module): persona_id → display location string or None; use unified_personas + LOCATION_MAPPINGS.

Load series title plan when available; compute series_subtitle; generate or read book title/subtitle per rules; pass series_subtitle into series metadata; keep current behavior when no plan.





tools/series_metadata_generator.py



Use series_subtitle from series_metadata when present for google_play.subtitle and description.





tools/phoenix_series_manager.py



Persist series_subtitle on series JSON if provided.





unified_personas.yaml



Optional: add location_overlay (display string) for personas that need a storefront location; otherwise derive from location_data.location.

8. Implementation order





Define and document schemas for series_title_plan.yaml and niche_brand_voice.yaml; add 1–2 example files.



Implement location resolver (persona_id → " in {location}" or ""), using unified_personas and existing LOCATION_MAPPINGS.

Phoenix series builder: plan lookup, use plan for series_name/series_subtitle and per-book title/subtitle; fallback to current dose template when no plan.



Series metadata: persist and use series_subtitle; update generate_series_description to use it.



Optional: add --use-series-title-engine path to generate titles when no plan exists and optionally write plan to artifacts.
